Huang San knew that he was no match for Li Jun, so he ran away quickly, sighing all the way: "I, the hero Huang San, am unlucky today!"

Li Jun flicked his hand casually, and with a punch, Huang San shouted "Ouch!" and fell far away. Xiao En was extremely surprised by the side!)

A Decaying Nation

— Borong

Recently, the newspapers have almost entirely become a portrayal of the observation of China. Although this portrayal is not very clear, making it impossible for us to make a thorough and in-depth observation of the entire national society, merely from the fragmentary words, after a little thought, it is enough to make us shudder. In this serious environment, how much more should we strive for self-improvement, deeply realizing our mission and future from reality? And further cultivate and enrich our strength as a preparation for the rejuvenation of the nation.

However, when we observe again, we should be even more surprised to discover that in this metropolis containing thousands of people, there are in fact two extremely different and opposing sides. On the one hand, the market is sluggish, shops are closing one after another, factories are stopping work, and the number of unemployed people is increasing day by day, but on the other hand, more and more movie theaters and dance halls are opening, and the import of luxury goods is increasing unabated. The entire society is indulging in this kind of decadent and hedonistic morbid atmosphere, not looking back at the past, not looking forward to the future, only pursuing momentary and insane enjoyment; the opposition of these two phenomena really shows the great crisis of the entire society. Everyone who lives in this society should give it some thought and consideration.

The aggression of imperial capitalism has bankrupted our rural economy, and medieval handicrafts are obviously unable to compete with the magical modern machinery. However, on the other hand, the newly rising national capitalism needs to be carefully nurtured, but the forces of imperialism have completely destroyed this glimmer of hope. What did they bring us? Sensual movies, champagne, expensive cigarettes, luxurious fabrics, cosmetics, and everything that leads us to destruction. Their mentality is that they are afraid of us striving for self-improvement, and they will use all means to inject these toxins into the blood of our nation. But our compatriots - especially those pampered upper-class Chinese - have not realized this. No, it's not that they don't realize it, it's just that they deliberately close their eyes to this serious phenomenon. Their families are foreign goods sales factories, their bodies are foreign goods advertisement racks, their entire lives are Westernized lives, and the entire concession has become a market of ancient Rome, licentiousness, extravagance, dissipation, laziness, and decadence. What qualifications do these soulless and lifeless people have to undertake the arduous and great task of revitalizing the nation? The aggression of imperialism is not terrible, what is terrible is our own decay. Because of our decay, imperialism is able to implement their conspiracies and poisonous strategies unscrupulously. We try to think about the suffering of our compatriots in the Northeast, the unrest and disasters in rural areas across the country, and the depth of the economic crisis in the big cities. Will we not let our lives take a sharp turn downwards, and firmly cultivate our own will? Reality is cruel. It is foolish to ignore reality, and it is cowardly to escape reality. Only those who face reality head-on and sincerely transform it are the strong in human life.

Our nation is like a patient groaning in bed. For this patient, delicious food is useless, and strong medicine is harmful. We need a tonic that strengthens the foundation and restores health.

Fiction

Siamese Ramblings (9)

— Yi Min

There are only two reasons why overseas Chinese education in Siam is not developed. First, tuition is too expensive. Schools there charge monthly fees, averaging four Siamese baht per month. A year is calculated as twelve months (fees must also be paid during winter and summer vacations), which is forty-eight yuan. Adding books, stationery, and various miscellaneous fees, it costs no less than seventy yuan per year. If calculated in Chinese currency, that would be one hundred and twenty to thirty yuan. This is still for day students. If it is a boarding student, it is even more shockingly expensive. After all, not every overseas Chinese is rich. How can they afford such expensive tuition? Second, there is the muddle-headedness of some parents, because most of them are illiterate and think that they can still get rich without reading, so why send their children to school? There are indeed quite a few people like this.

Bangkok has the largest number of overseas Chinese, so education is also more developed than in the interior. Among them, the earliest established schools are Guangzhao of Guangdong, Peiying of Chaozhou, Yumin of Qiongzhou, Jinde of Hakka, and Peiyuan of Minnan. However, these so-called public schools all have a common problem, which is that the school board interferes with school administration. Not only is the hiring and firing of teachers decided by them, but even very minor things such as adding a few books to the library must be approved by them. It is a welcome phenomenon that the school board is willing to take responsibility, but to put it bluntly, most of them are laymen when it comes to education. So although the finances are very sufficient, they can't achieve anything good.

In recent years, private schools have developed very quickly. Because they are not constrained by the school board, and the teachers are mostly young people who have gone from the country, they are very energetic. Among them, the most hardworking are Xinmin, Chongshi,魂, and the newly established Huazhong and Nanyang last year. In addition to humbly studying and constantly improving in teaching, they have indeed made considerable contributions to the entire overseas Chinese community. For example, the implementation of co-education has had very good results. In recent years, the atmosphere of the overseas Chinese community has become more open than before, and the contribution of private schools is not small.

Film Supplement

Regarding "The Evaluation of Flames" and Others (Part Two)

— Ling He

Secondly, Mr. Mu Tian knows full well that merely cursing film writers is not enough to destroy Chinese cinema. He must further sweep away all film criticism to achieve "universal peace." Therefore, he "puts on a mysteriously arrogant face" and "inflicts great destruction."

Regarding these claims, I only wish to respond with facts. To the best of my recollection, when "The Iron Plate Red Tears Record" was released, none of the film reviews were so "harsh" as to be "worthless." We did not, as he claims, think that simply because "the screenwriter and director went to the rural areas of Sichuan to find material, instead of always seeking the easiest route and drawing material from the narrow Jiangnan region," it was "worthy of excessive praise." On the contrary, we believed that the playwright's ability to grasp the theme of opposing feudal forces and exorbitant taxes was precisely what made "The Iron Plate Red Tears Record" so commendable. However, Mr. Mu Tian deliberately quibbles, saying it was "worthless," but I really don't know what he means. Of course, we know that he is an artist, and he should not overlook the fact that the director did not use better expressive methods to artistically express the substantial content of the film, which is a shortcoming that most film critics deeply regretted. However, he, without knowing on what basis, "actually brought out the spirit of a chivalrous knight from minor operas... fighting injustice."

Similarly, regarding "Flames," I know that none of the criticisms in the various newspapers were "so lenient as to be close to singing its praises." They realistically pointed out its merits and its flaws. Unlike Mr. Mu Tian, who only saw "A Zhen's watery eyes that are really flirtatious to 200 percent" and "growing vegetables and flirting," and overlooked the film's encouragement of millions of suffering people to extinguish the flames of imperialism. Only the so-called "avant-garde great poet" has such a foul-mouthed intention and courage to declare it "worthless". Here, I don't understand who "doesn't know where the progressiveness of the work lies? Where the regressiveness of the work lies?" In fact, he is "obstructing good tendencies." Furthermore, as for "Spring Silkworms," at that time, some people inevitably thought that because expectations were so high, there was no need to praise the good points, which were universally known, and that the shortcomings had to be fully pointed out to encourage the author to improve. This should be a very clear and obvious fact. Is there any malice in it? However, when we self-critically discovered our own mistakes, that is, when we realized that it caused the audience to misunderstand, we very humbly and sincerely corrected our own mistakes. Is this what is called "the joke of re-evaluation"? I believe that "to correct one's mistakes is better than not correcting them." Moreover, the lack of popularity of "Spring Silkworms" and its lack of dramatic elements have become a widely recognized conclusion. If, as he advocates, it were exactly the same as the original novel, only noble and progressive poets could understand it.

As for the criticism of Don Quixote, which pains him the most, it is even more contrary to the facts. Isn't that so? He scolds "Chinese film critics for their lack of common sense, not even knowing the A.B.C. of world literature," unlike him, who "bought a book on the development of European literature" and even "looked at it for twenty or thirty minutes," so he "understood the gist of it." But in fact, the criticisms in the various newspapers at that time almost all used his "understanding of the gist" of the development of European literature as a reference. The facts are all there. Why does he have to quibble like this? Moreover, at that time, a film magazine even published a special issue about it, with introductions from various aspects. Does Mr. Mu Tian not read the newspapers? I believe he should "at least have common sense."

Finally, what I want to say is that Mr. Hou Feng seems to be objectively helping him strike at the Chinese film criticism that the environment wants to strike at, which is no different from the behavior of the likes of Gao Ming. I really admire their crusading courage. But I believe that Chinese film criticism will never be destroyed because of this. Chinese film criticism will always help Chinese films break free from all shackles and move forward! (The End)

Film Review

Review of "Brief Moment"

— Ling He

Brief Moment │ Nanjing │

Ever since Warner Bros.' "I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang," a painful and indignant impression has always lingered in my mind. Indeed, that is a remarkable masterpiece in the Hollywood film kingdom. I think all the audiences who have seen that work should not deny its great existence. Therefore, I sincerely recommend "I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang" to the audience in Shanghai, because it is a film worth appreciating, and you will inevitably gain many valuable things from it.

However, I think when we watch the above film and then watch other mediocre American films, we may feel disappointed. At least for me, I can't help but be disappointed in Columbia Pictures' "Brief Moment" when compared to it.

Of course, this is by no means an unfounded subjective assertion. In fact, in terms of content evaluation, it is a rather empty film that lacks seriousness. When I saw "End" on the screen, I immediately thought of the common saying "The prodigal son who returns is worth more than gold." Indeed, the whole story can be summarized in this sentence, and what the playwright emphasizes is only explaining the prodigal son's return, mainly to repay the wife's love.

The story is constructed like this: A banker's son, who spends money like water, falls in love with a singer and surprisingly abandons the so-called class concept and marries her. Because he spends his days chasing after singing stages and dance halls, the singer repeatedly advises him in vain, and eventually divorces him. Afterwards, the son deeply feels his wife's deep affection and strives to reform himself. Thus, they are reunited, and the husband and wife are as loving as before.

What is the theme here? Obviously, young masters should engage in proper business and not be obsessed with leisure. In the eyes of a virtuous wife and good mother, it is for the sake of her husband's own future, while for the young master himself, it is for the sake of his wife's persuasion. This is undoubtedly an extremely old worldview, a so-called truism that is not annoying, but also does not make people feel excited. If this also has its social significance, then the playwright is simply teaching wealthy children not to be self-destructive in order to maintain the existence of their class interests. However, this is just a pathetic illusion.

Furthermore, does this film have any authenticity? Well, in depicting the extravagant lives of Ladies and Gentlemen, it does a pretty good job of drawing a true outline. Their lives consist of dancing, champagne, women, jazz music, horse racing, ice skating, driving around in cars, sightseeing, roulette gambling. . . . . . They don't understand what life is, they hate work and they don't have to work. They have plenty of money, just sign a name on the checkbook. In case of a deficit, their parents and brothers will naturally supply them. Under these circumstances, will they realize that they should serve society? We see that when the young master is copying accounts in his father's bank, he cannot endure hardship and eventually goes to watch horse races.

However, this only describes a little of their lives. The playwright absolutely does not unfold the collapse of the old or the growth of the new in their life sketches. We cannot feel that it is an inevitable phenomenon of collapse here. Thus, the playwright carries out his task. He not only does not strike at their upper class, but also vigorously maintains their existence. If someone says that when the young master is looking for a job and is constantly turned down, does this reflect the fear of unemployment? Then, I can answer that it is only to write about the young master's determination to do good, so it is okay to do that.

The director is more or less smooth in narrating a story. But the occasional misuse of the camera proves his lack of cultivation. Neither the male nor female lead has any special talents. In particular, the male lead is frivolous, exaggerated, and even more childish, while the female lead is just not good enough.

After Watching the Little Women Preview

— Zheng Xiaocang

At the invitation of the Great Shanghai Theater, I had the pleasure of seeing the film "Little Women," which I had been looking forward to for months. Of course, having experienced and expressed the psychology of each character in the play, I feel an even closer connection. However, those who were moved by the unrestrained and profound emotions have probably already become the Jiangzhou Sima in the audience.

The script adapts some of the plots from the original book ("Little Women and Good Wives," which has been combined into one book in the United States), but it has already become a grand spectacle. Katharine Hepburn plays the central figure with great detail. The other people also express their individuality. The performance of Aunt March is especially hilarious, which is enough to relieve the tension of emotions. I know that many people who have read my translation of "Little Women and Good Wives" are delighted to hear of the arrival of this film and are eager to see it. After watching the film, they can review the plots in the book, and they can see the characteristics of literary art and screen art, and see that they do not take away from each other but actually complement each other. In recent months, I have often seen American magazines, such as "Literary Digest" and "Parents Magazine," praising this film. Many film review agencies in the United States have praised it, saying that it has educational value. I feel today that it is indeed worthy of praise. I think elementary school students can also appreciate it (in addition to the four "Little Women," there are also many scenes of children), not to mention those who have received secondary education or above. This kind of sincere, pure, and uplifting film, families and schools should introduce it to their children, because the original books "Little Women and Good Wives" are not only literary readings, but also educational readings.

Film Novel

The Record of War Drums and Swords (Nana (5)

— Yin Ming

In terms of position, Antri is a colonel, while George is only a major, so Antri said in the tone of a superior ordering a subordinate: "You will immediately return to the camp to report. I will soon have an order sent to your officer there. Your vacation has been cancelled. Now you are serving the Royal Family, and you must immediately take the entire team to Acquilina." George shouted: "Antri! How can you treat me like this?" Antri said: "George, it is for the sake of loving you that I prevent you from falling for life." He said, returning to his room to get dressed.

Not long after, Antri was dressed, and he ran over to see Nana. At that time, Nana was still lying on the bed. Hearing someone knock on the door, she was a little unhappy to pay attention, but the knocking became tighter and louder. Nana said slowly, "Go away, I still want to sleep." Antri said outside the door: "I am Colonel Mu Fa. I want to see you immediately." Su Yi was awakened, and she led Antri into Nana's room. Antri entered the room, and seeing that Nana had not gotten up, he put a small package on her bed. Nana asked him what it was. Antri said: "This is the full price you pay for all the love my brother has for you. There are 50,000 francs in it. With this money, you don't have to be with him anymore. You see, I gave it to you without you even asking. My brother is not allowed to go to you from now on. He is leaving for Acquilina at midnight tonight. I can't let him lightly throw away his life's future." Nana asked: "Does George know that you came to me?" Antri said: "Of course not. But I did this entirely for the sake of loving him." Antri finished speaking and was about to leave, but Nana stopped him. She jumped out of bed. She stood in front of Antri and said: "I didn't want to discuss this matter with you narrow-minded people." Her voice was extremely cold, filled with suppressed anger. "But, my dear Colonel, you need to know that the reason why you did this is not because you love your brother. I have also seen people like you. They live the lives of monks for their entire lives. Look at me! I belong to George, I love him. He also loves me. Now you want to punish your brother, because what I have, you don't even understand. This is where you are different. Now to prove that what I said is not wrong . . . . . ." She finished speaking and kissed Antri very hard on the lips. "Now, my dear Colonel," Nana laughed coldly after kissing him, "you know what kind of hypocrite you are. For many years, your brain has been deceiving your body . . . . . . hehe . . . . . . You love your brother. God knows. Take your money and get out! I will hate myself for the rest of my life for ever touching you!" Antri suffered this great insult and left with his head down. On Nana's side, she didn't believe that George would really leave her, so she forgot about Antri's visit after it was over and didn't think it was a big deal. But at dusk, a carriage came and stopped in front of her door, loaded with luggage. George jumped out of the carriage. Nana was stunned by this scene. He came in. He hugged her. He told her very calmly and straightforwardly that he was going on a long journey. (To be continued)
